                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-04-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature America, Inc."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The domestication of wild emmer wheat led to the selection of modern durum wheat, grown mainly for pasta production. We describe the 10.45&#8201;gigabase (Gb) assembly of the genome of durum wheat cultivar Svevo. The assembly enabled genome-wide genetic diversity analyses revealing the changes imposed by thousands of years of empirical selection and breeding. Regions exhibiting strong signatures of genetic divergence associated with domestication and breeding were widespread in the genome with several major diversity losses in the pericentromeric regions. A locus on chromosome 5B carries a gene encoding a metal transporter (TdHMA3-B1) with a non-functional variant causing high accumulation of cadmium in grain. The high-cadmium allele, widespread among durum cultivars but undetected in wild emmer accessions, increased in frequency from domesticated emmer to modern durum wheat. The rapid cloning of TdHMA3-B1 rescues a wild beneficial allele and demonstrates the practical use of the Svevo genome for wheat improvement. Genome assembly of durum wheat cultivar Svevo enables genome-wide genetic diversity analyses highlighting modifications imposed by thousands of years of empirical selection and breeding."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3011-8731"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3011-8731</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 0943 9907, grid.418934.3, Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research, Gatersleben, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Iago-Hale" data-author-popup="auth-Iago-Hale">Iago Hale</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-8558-7861"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-8558-7861</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff20">20</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of New Hampshire" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2192 7145, grid.167436.1, Department of Agriculture, Nutrition, and Food Systems, University of New Hampshire, Durham, NH, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Assaf-Distelfeld" data-author-popup="auth-Assaf-Distelfeld">Assaf Distelfeld</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff11">11</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Tel Aviv University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1937 0546, grid.12136.37, School of Plant Sciences and Food Security, Tel Aviv University, Tel Aviv, Israel" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Matthew_J_-Hayden" data-author-popup="auth-Matthew_J_-Hayden">Matthew J. Hayden</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3117-5500"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3117-5500</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a>,<a href="#Aff26">26</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Agribio Centre for AgriBioscience" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0407 2669, grid.452283.a, Agriculture Victoria, Agribio Centre for AgriBioscience, Bundoora, Victoria, Australia" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="La Trobe University" class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6484-1077"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6484-1077</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a>,<a href="#Aff27">27</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Plant Genome and Systems Biology" /><meta itemprop="address" content="Helmholtz Zentrum München, Plant Genome and Systems Biology, Neuherberg, Germany" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Technical University Munich" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000123222966, grid.6936.a, School of Life Sciences Weihenstephan, Technical University Munich, Freising, Germany" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na2">na2</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Aldo-Ceriotti" data-author-popup="auth-Aldo-Ceriotti" data-corresp-id="c2">Aldo Ceriotti<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide">

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-6067-4600"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-6067-4600</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff8">8</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="CREA—Research Centre for Genomics and Bioinformatics" /><meta itemprop="address" content="CREA—Research Centre for Genomics and Bioinformatics, Fiorenzuola d’Arda, Italy" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na2">na2</a></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The domestication of wild emmer wheat led to the selection of modern durum wheat, grown mainly for pasta production. We describe the 10.45 gigabase (Gb) assembly of the genome of durum wheat cultivar Svevo. The assembly enabled genome-wide genetic diversity analyses revealing the changes imposed by thousands of years of empirical selection and breeding. Regions exhibiting strong signatures of genetic divergence associated with domestication and breeding were widespread in the genome with several major diversity losses in the pericentromeric regions. A locus on chromosome 5B carries a gene encoding a metal transporter (<i>TdHMA3-B1</i>) with a non-functional variant causing high accumulation of cadmium in grain. The high-cadmium allele, widespread among durum cultivars but undetected in wild emmer accessions, increased in frequency from domesticated emmer to modern durum wheat. The rapid cloning of <i>TdHMA3-B1</i> rescues a wild beneficial allele and demonstrates the practical use of the Svevo genome for wheat improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Durum wheat (DW), <i>Triticum turgidum</i> L. ssp. <i>durum</i> (Desf.) Husn., genome BBAA, is a cereal grain mainly used for pasta production and evolved from domesticated emmer wheat (DEW), <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>dicoccum</i> (Schrank ex Schübl.) Thell. DEW itself derived from wild emmer wheat (WEW), <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>dicoccoides</i> (Körn. ex Asch. &amp; Graebn.) Thell., in the Fertile Crescent about 10,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862–1866 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1631">1</a></sup>. Although the first evidence of DW dates to 6,500–7,500 years ago, DW became established as a prominent crop only 1,500–2,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Faris, J. Genomics of Plant Genetic Resources Ch. 18 (Springer, 2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1635">2</a></sup>. Thus, the human-driven tetraploid wheat evolution process is the result of domestication (WEW to DEW), continued evolution under domestication (DEW to durum wheat landraces, DWL) and breeding improvement from DWL to modern durum wheat cultivars (DWC).</p><p>Wild relatives of modern crop plants can serve as sources of valuable genetic diversity for various traits (for example, disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Steffenson, B. J. et al. A walk on the wild side: mining wild wheat and barley collections for rust resistance genes. Aust. J. Agric. Res. 58, 532–544 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1642">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ellis, J. G., Lagudah, E. S., Spielmeyer, W. &amp; Dodds, P. N. The past, present and future of breeding rust resistant wheat. Front. Plant Sci. 5, 641 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1645">4</a></sup> and nutritional quality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Uauy, C., Distelfeld, A., Fahima, T., Blechl, A. &amp; Dubcovsky, J. A. NAC gene regulating senescence improves grain protein, zinc and iron content in wheat. Science 314, 1298–1300 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1649">5</a></sup>). Comprehensive comparative genomic analyses between cultivated crops and wild progenitors is a key strategy to detect novel beneficial alleles and structural variations that could constrain breeding efforts, as well as to understand the broader genetic consequences of evolution and selection history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Gaut, B. S. Evolution is an experiment: assessing parallelism in crop domestication and experimental evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 1661–1671 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1653">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Brozynska, M., Furtado, A. &amp; Henry, R. J. Genomics of crop wild relatives: expanding the gene pool for crop improvement. Plant Biotech. J. 14, 1070–1085 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1656">7</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we report the fully assembled genome of the modern DW cultivar (cv.) Svevo and provide a genome-wide account of modifications imposed by thousands of years of empirical selection and breeding. This was achieved by comparing the Svevo genome with the assembled genome of WEW accession Zavitan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1663">8</a></sup> and through a survey of the genetic diversity and selection signatures in a Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection consisting of 1,856 accessions. A region bearing a signature of historic selection co-locates with <i>Cdu-B1</i>, a quantitative trait locus (QTL) spanning 0.7 cM on chromosome 5B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Wiebe, K. et al. Targeted mapping of Cdu1, a major locus regulating grain cadmium concentration in durum wheat (Triticum turgidum L. var durum). Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 1047–1058 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1670">9</a></sup> known to control cadmium (Cd) accumulation in the grain. Identification of the gene(s) responsible for <i>Cdu-B1</i> has been hampered by the large and repetitive nature of the DW genome and the low recombination rate in the region of interest. The efficient, genome-enabled dissection of the <i>Cdu-B1</i> locus reported here demonstrates the value of the Svevo genome assembly for wheat improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">The durum wheat reference genome</h3><p>The Svevo genome sequence was assembled de novo using protocols previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1692">8</a></sup> and its main features are illustrated in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig1">1</a>. After sequencing (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">1</a>) and assembly, the scaffolds (length of the shortest contig needed to cover 50% of the genome (N50) = 6.0 megabases (Mb); Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">2</a>) were ordered and oriented using the Svevo × Zavitan genetic map as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Avni, R. et al. Ultra-dense genetic map of durum wheat×wild emmer wheat developed using the 90K iSelect SNP genotyping assay. Mol. Breed. 34, 1549–1562 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1705">10</a></sup>. Thereafter, chromosome conformation capture sequencing (Hi-C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1710">11</a></sup> resulted in a set of pseudomolecules (9.96 Gb; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">3</a>) corresponding to the 14 chromosomes of DW and one group of unassigned scaffolds (499 Mb). The pseudomolecules encompass 95.3% of the assembled sequences and have 90% of the scaffolds oriented. Alignment of the DW genome with high-density SNP genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Maccaferri, M. et al. A high-density, SNP-based consensus map of tetraploid wheat as a bridge to integrate durum and bread wheat genomics and breeding. Plant Biotech. J. 13, 648–663 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1717">12</a></sup> showed highly recombinogenic distal chromosome regions exhibiting an almost linear relationship between genetic and physical distance (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">1</a>). These regions account for about 22% of the genome with an average recombination rate of 1.8 Mb cM<sup>−1</sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">4</a>). In contrast, large pericentromeric regions are nearly devoid of recombination and represent about 44% of the genome, with a mean recombination rate of 107 Mb cM<sup>−1</sup>. Annotation of the Svevo genome led to the identification of 66,559 high confidence (HC) genes, 90.5% of which exhibited detectable evidence of expression in at least one of the 21 RNA-seq datasets listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">5</a>. A detailed description of the DW genome is presented in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> (Sections 1.1 and 2.1). Projection onto the DW genome of 2,191 previously reported QTLs resulted in a full meta-QTL analysis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">6b</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM3">1</a>), revealing a QTL density distribution that closely mirrors the gene density distribution (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">7</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig1">1d,e</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Structural, functional and conserved synteny landscape of the DW genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Structural, functional and conserved synteny landscape of the DW genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="800" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Tracks from outside to inside. <b>a</b>, Chromosome name and size (100 Mb tick size, arms differentiated by gray shading). <b>b</b>, Density of WEW HC gene models (HC; 0–25 genes per Mb). <b>c</b>, Links connecting homologous genes between WEW and DW. <b>d</b>, Density of DW HC gene models (0–22 genes per Mb). <b>e</b>, Location of published QTLs. <b>f</b>, <i>k</i>-mer frequencies. <b>g</b>, Long terminal repeat (LTR)-retrotransposon density. <b>h</b>, DNA transposon frequency. <b>i</b>, Mean expression of HC genes calculated as log(FPKM + 1) of the mean expression value of all conditions (range 1.6–8.2). Links in center connect homoeologous genes between subgenomes; blue links between homoeologous chromosomes and green links between large translocated regions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparison between Svevo and Zavitan genomes</h3><p>To gain insights into short-term evolutionary changes, we compared the genome divergence between the modern DW cultivar Svevo and the WEW accession Zavitan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1810">8</a></sup>. The comparison revealed strong overall synteny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig1">1c</a>) with high similarity in total HC gene number (DW 66,559; WEW 67,182; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">8</a>), chromosome structure and transposable element composition (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">9</a>). We identified syntenic LTR-retrotransposon insertions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">2</a>) not yet subjected to the rapid transposable element turnover of the intergenic space<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862–1866 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1827">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wicker, T. et al. Impact of transposable elements on genome structure and evolution in bread wheat. Genome Biol. 19, 103 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1830">13</a></sup> because of the relatively short separation time between Svevo and Zavitan. To monitor structural variations in the HC gene set, including minor changes that might have been generated during the short evolutionary timespan, a graph-based sequence clustering of all Svevo and Zavitan HC genes (in total 133,741) was undertaken. Stringent clustering (alignment e value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>, overlap &gt; 75% and identity &gt; 75%) grouped only highly similar gene models in the same cluster. This approach produced 36,434 unigene groups, 79% (28,794) of which were clusters with at least two members, while 21% (7,640) contained only singletons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">10</a>). The main scenarios for conserved and variable genes are summarized in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig2">2a</a>. The most frequent cluster configuration is made of two homoeologous gene copies per genome (one per A and B subgenomes), which occurs in 35% of all unigene groups. Altogether, the unigene groups with balanced copy numbers for Svevo and Zavitan represent up to 60% of all unigenes and involve 63% of all Svevo genes. The remaining 40% of unigenes (14,660) display asymmetric numbers of intact, full-length genes between Svevo and Zavitan (we named this intact gene number variation). Since the unigene classification is on the basis of HC genes, any mutation leading to a frameshift and/or premature stop codon that rules out a gene from the HC class in Svevo or in Zavitan, results in an asymmetric unigene distribution. For at least two-thirds of the unigenes displaying variation of intact gene number, counterparts for the missing copies can still be found in LC or pseudogene class. The complete gene loss caused by large structural variations was responsible for asymmetric gene distribution in only one-third of the cases. Among the unbalanced gene clusters, there are 6,120 mixed clusters with copies from both genomes, subdivided into more Svevo members or more Zavitan members, as well as 4,313 and 4,227 lineage-specific unigene groups (mostly singleton genes) in Svevo and Zavitan, respectively, which have no close homoeolog in the HC gene set of the other accession. A detailed example of the type of variation leading to intact gene number variation is given for the lineage-specific unigenes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">10a</a>). In Svevo, this class includes 4,811 genes that represent 7.2% of all HC genes, a value similar to the 5% found after the comparison between two cultivars in a recent pangenome study of hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Montenegro, J. D. et al. The pangenome of hexaploid bread wheat. Plant J. 90, 1007–1013 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1845">14</a></sup>. When the Svevo-specific genes were mapped onto the Zavitan genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">10b</a>), 1,493 genes (31%) were not found on the Zavitan sequence, 1,225 (26%) correspond to shorter counterparts of annotated Zavitan HC genes and 1,095 (23%) were annotated as low confidence (LC) genes or pseudogenes. The remaining 965 (20%, that is, 1.4% of all Svevo HC genes) map to unannotated regions and are candidates for genes missed in the automated annotation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparison of the Svevo and Zavitan gene space."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparison of the Svevo and Zavitan gene space.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="630" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Main unigene group scenarios from co-clustering of Svevo and Zavitan HC genes. The diagram depicts the most common or typical scenarios, only HC intact genes were considered (CNV, copy number variation; CDS, coding sequence). <b>b</b>, Intact gene number variations. Each dot represents a gene cluster consisting of DW (<i>x</i> axis) and WEW (<i>y</i> axis) genes. Dots on the diagonal represent clusters with identical member numbers from both accessions. Functional predictions for some groups of genes with pronounced differences in member numbers are annotated on the diagram. <b>c</b>, Relative distance of DW genes from the centromere separated by gene cluster type. Proportionately more unigenes displaying intact gene number variation than balanced groups are observed towards the ends of the chromosome. HC genes unique to Svevo or Zavitan (not shown) are most highly represented at the ends of the chromosomes, with the median (black line) furthest from the centromere. Shape width represents the relative gene frequency.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Loss and gain events can occur from ancestral four-member unigene clusters with one gene per A and B subgenome of both Svevo and Zavitan. One loss would result in a three-member unigene cluster, with one subgenome location missing. A total of 1,121 clusters with one lost Svevo member were found. The reverse situation with one lost member from Zavitan was found 852 times. The presumed HC gene losses are located predominantly in the more distal chromosomal regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">3a</a>). A gain event would result in clusters with at least five members with one subgenome carrying two members. This condition was found 472 times with Svevo gains and 503 times with Zavitan gains. Most of the gains are located on the same chromosome indicating tandem gene duplication as the prevailing mechanism (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">3b</a>).</p><p>Functional categories associated with relevant copy number differences between the two accessions are highlighted in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig2">2b</a>. A statistical analysis for gene ontology over-representation revealed that specific functions can be classified as: differentially enriched in Svevo, differentially enriched in Zavitan, balanced and Svevo-specific unigene groups (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">3c</a>). The balanced gene groups are enriched for a multitude of regulatory functions and indicate a stronger sensitivity to gene dosage effects for the main regulatory networks. Unbalanced unigene groups with more Svevo members and Svevo unique groups are enriched for functions involving protein phosphorylation, for example kinases, which are known to trigger signal transduction cascades in response to environmental cues. The distal highly recombinogenic regions of chromosomes are enriched in unigenes displaying variation of intact gene number (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig2">2c</a>), contain most of the known QTLs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig1">1e</a>) and the HC genes display a reduced expression breadth (that is, average expression value across all tissue/treatment conditions, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig1">1i</a>). This indicates the presence of an increased number of condition-specific genes. The less dynamic interstitial regions contain a greater number of balanced gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig2">2c</a>). Here the genes are expressed in nearly all conditions, indicative of an enrichment in housekeeping genes that is consistent with reports from the barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1921">11</a></sup> and bread wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding through a fully annotated and anchored reference genome sequence. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1925">15</a></sup> genomes. The positive correlation between recombination rate and DNA variants supports previous evidence that higher recombination rates and illegitimate recombination are drivers for tandem duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Saintenac, C., Jiang, D. &amp; Akhunov, E. D. Targeted analysis of nucleotide and copy number variation by exon capture in allotetraploid wheat genome. Genome Biol. 12, R88 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1929">16</a></sup>.</p><p>The balanced copy number groups contain much longer genes (median 1,152 base pairs (bp)) than the groups displaying variation of intact gene number (median 879 bp; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig2">2a</a>). The highest median gene lengths are found in groups with two copies in each genome (1,242 bp), whereas the lowest are among the unique genes (Svevo, 735 bp; Zavitan, 768 bp) and, surprisingly, in groups with one copy in each genome (756 bp). Such a pronounced shift towards shorter genes indicates an ongoing gene decay by frameshifts and mutations leading to premature stop codons. Collectively, the relatively high number of genes undergoing degeneration could be a consequence of more freedom for gene loss facilitated by the functional redundancy of the tetraploid genome state.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Germplasm structure and phylogenetic relationships</h3><p>The wheat iSelect 90K SNP Infinium assay<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, S. et al. Characterization of polyploid wheat genomic diversity using a high-density 90 000 single nucleotide polymorphism array. Plant Biotech. J. 12, 787–796 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1947">17</a></sup> was used to genotype the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection consisting of 1,856 accessions representing the four main germplasm groups involved in tetraploid wheat domestication history and breeding: WEW, DEW, DWL and DWC (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">11</a>). A set of 17,340 SNPs (non-redundant, genetically and physically mapped, subgenome-specific Mendelian loci) was used for analysis of genetic diversity, population structure and identification of the selection signatures. Four non-hierarchical clustering analyses (DAPC, sNMF, ADMIXTURE, fineSTRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jombart, T., Devillard, S. &amp; Balloux, F. Discriminant analysis of principal components: a new method for the analysis of genetically structured populations. BMC Genet. 11, 94 (2010)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1954">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Frichot, E., Mathieu, F., Trouillon, T., Bouchard, G. &amp; François, O. Fast and efficient estimation of individual ancestry coefficients. Genetics 196, 973–983 (2014)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1954_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Alexander, D. H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1954_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lawson, D. J., Hellenthal, G., Myers, S. &amp; Falush, D. Inference of population structure using dense haplotype data. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002453 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39583e1957">21</a></sup>), principal component analysis, and pairwise dissimilarity analysis on the basis of neighbor joining generated global and highly concordant pictures of the genetic relationships among taxa and populations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Datasets <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM4">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM5">3</a>). WEW, DEW, DWL and DWC clearly separated in the neighbor joining tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig3">3a</a>), a result indicative of strong demographic and founder effects and little evidences for polyphyletic origin. Principal component analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig3">3b</a>) illustrates the broad genetic diversity of DEW, while DWC showed a comparatively limited genetic diversity and a close relationship to a specific DWL population. DEW and DWL from Ethiopia, <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>turanicum</i> and <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>carthlicum</i> were genetically isolated.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Tetraploid germplasm structure and phylogenetic relationships."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Tetraploid germplasm structure and phylogenetic relationships.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="545" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Neighbor joining tree from Nei’s genetic distances among the 1,856 accessions of the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection. Genetic distances were computed from a set of 5,775 whole-genome linkage disquilibrium–pruned (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.5) SNPs. Correspondence between branches and main tetraploid wheat taxa/populations on the basis of ADMIXTURE and other population structure analyses are indicated by color code with the exception of <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>karamychevii</i> and ssp. <i>isphahanicum</i> that are indicated directly on the graph. Significances were estimated through 1,000 bootstrap resampling. <b>b</b>, Principal component analysis plot of the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection on the basis of genome-wide pairwise distances calculated on the basis of linkage disquilibrium-pruned SNPs. <b>c</b>, ADMIXTURE analyses of the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection with <i>k</i> (number of populations assumed for the analysis) from 2 to 20.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>ADMIXTURE analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig3">3c</a>) showed that WEW and DEW have highly structured genetic diversity even at high <i>k</i> values, while DWL showed a high rate of admixture already at low <i>k</i> values. WEW germplasm was divided into two main populations from North Eastern Fertile Crescent and Southern Levant (WEW-NE and WEW-SL, respectively). WEW-NE was further divided into several populations from Turkey, Iran and Iraq, while WEW-SL included distinct populations from Israel (3), Jordan, Syria and Lebanon (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">4</a>). DEW and DWL germplasm was characterized by a similar though independent radial dispersal pattern: Northern-to-Southern Fertile Crescent and from Fertile Crescent to Mediterranean basin (Western), Greece to Balkans (Western), Iran to Transcaucasia (Eastern) and Oman to India and Ethiopia. The germplasm belonging to DEW and DWL was subdivided in six main populations each, while all DWC clustered to a further distinct group that represents a wide branch of the durum North African and Turkey to Transcaucasian landrace populations (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>After removing the accessions with a high level of admixture, the genetic relationships among the main tetraploid germplasm groups were further investigated using hierarchical analysis of variance (ANOVA), by computing the pairwise divergence index (or fixation index) <i>F</i><sub>st</sub> and Nei’s genetic distances (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig4">4</a>), and by generating population-based whole-genome phylogenetic trees (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">5</a>). The results confirm the radial dispersal patterns already reported and indicate the WEW-NE from Turkey as the most probable ancestor of all DEW populations (<i>F</i><sub>st</sub> and genetic distance values consistently lower for all WEW-DEW pairs). Two DEW populations from Southern Levant Fertile Crescent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig4">4</a>) showed the closest relationship to all DWL populations (except <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>turanicum</i>), while the DWC germplasm was mostly related to the two DWL populations from North Africa and Transcaucasia (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig4">4</a>). The Ethiopian and <i>T. turgidum</i> ssp. <i>turanicum</i> populations were the most differentiated among the DWL germplasm and their contribution to the modern durum varieties was minimal.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Summary of Nei’s genetic distances GDst (above diagonal) and pairwise Fst (below diagonal) between main tetraploid wheat populations."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Summary of Nei’s genetic distances GD<sub>st</sub> (above diagonal) and pairwise <i>F</i><sub>st</sub> (below diagonal) between main tetraploid wheat populations.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="385" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Diagonal numbers represent within-population genetic diversity (expected heterozygosity) values. Only low-admixture accessions were used (Q-membership higher than 0.5 for WEW, DEW; Q-membership higher than 0.4 for DWL, DWC). Statistics were estimated with 5,775 linkage disequilibrium-pruned (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.5) SNPs. WEW-NE, WEW from the North Eastern Fertile Crescent, Turkey, Iran and Iraq; WEW-SL, WEW from Southern Levant including Lebanon, Syria, Israel and Jordan; DEW-T-TRC-IRN, DEW from Turkey to Transcaucasia and Iran; DEW-T-BLK, DEW from Turkey to the Balkans; DEW-SthEU, DEW spread in Southern Mediterranean areas; DEW-SL-EU1, DEW from Southern Levant Fertile Crescent to Europe (population 1); DEW-SL-EU2, DEW from Southern Levant Fertile Crescent to Europe (population 2); DEW-ETH, DEW from Oman, India and Ethiopia; DWL-SL-NA, DWL from Southern Levant Fertile Crescent to North Africa and Iberia; DWL-GRC-BLK, DWL from Greece to Balkans; DWL-T-TRC, DWL from Turkey to Transcaucasia; DWL-T-FC, DWL diffused in Turkey to the whole Fertile Crescent; DWL-TRN, <i>T. turanicum</i>; DWL-ETH, DWL from Ethiopia; DWC-DRY, DWC from Italian and ICARDA breeding programs adapted to dryland areas; DWC-ITLY, DWC from Italy; DWC-CIM70, DWC from the wide adaptation, temperate-adapted photoperiod insensitive CIMMYT and ICARDA germplasm bred in the 1970s; DWC-CIM80, DWC from the high-yielding CIMMYT germplasm bred in the 1980s; DWC-AMR, DWC from the photoperiod-sensitive North American and French germplasm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Diversity reduction and signature of selection</h3><p>The pattern of diversity for each germplasm group was assessed through a SNP-based gene diversity index<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Nei, M. Analysis of gene diversity in subdivided populations. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 12, 3321–3323 (1973)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39583e2132">22</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig5">5a</a>). WEW showed the highest average diversity with only two pericentromeric regions (chromosomes 2A and 4A) with a lower than average diversity. Thus, WEW provides a valuable reference for assessing the reduction of diversity associated with domestication and breeding in tetraploid wheat. Compared to WEW, each of the subsequently domesticated/improved germplasm group showed several strong diversity depletions that arose independently and were progressively consolidated through domestication and breeding. With few exceptions, the diversity depletions that occurred in the early transition (WEW to DEW, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig5">5b</a> or DEW to DWL, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig6">6a</a>) are confirmed or even reinforced in the subsequent ones (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig6">6a,b</a>). Consequently, the genome of DWC is characterized by numerous regions showing near-fixation of allelic diversity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#Fig6">6b</a>). We applied five different metrics to detect selection signatures: diversity reduction index (DRI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Pankin, A., Altmüller, J., Becker, C. &amp; von Korff, M. Targeted resequencing reveals genomic signatures of barley domestication. New Phytol. 218, 1247–1259 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39583e2152">23</a></sup>), single site divergence index (<i>F</i><sub>st</sub>; ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Holsinger, K. E. &amp; Weir, B. S. Genetics in geographically structured populations: defining, estimating and interpreting FST. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 639–650 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39583e2160">24</a></sup>), haplotype-based frequency differentiation index (hapFLK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Fariello, M. I., Boitard, S., Naya, H., SanCristobal, M. &amp; Servin, B. Detecting signatures of selection through haplotype differentiation among hierarchically structured populations. Genetics 193, 929–941 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39583e2164">25</a></sup>), cross-population extended haplotype homozygosity (XP-EHH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Sabeti, P. C. et al. Genome-wide detection and characterization of positive selection in human populations. Nature 449, 913–918 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39583e2169">26</a></sup>), and spatial pattern of site frequency spectrum (XP-CLR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Chen, H., Patterson, N. &amp; Reich, D. Population differentiation as a test for selective sweeps. Genome Res. 20, 393–402 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39583e2173">27</a></sup>). Genomic regions supported by one or more indexes were considered as putative signatures of selection. Frequently, two or more indexes occurred in overlapping regions, hereafter referred as selection clusters. In total, 104 pericentromeric (average size 107.7 Mb) and 350 non-pericentromeric (average size 11.4 Mb) clusters were identified in one, two or three transitions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genome-wide analysis of SNP diversity in the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection and cross-population selection signatures from wild to domesticated emmer transition (WEW to DEW) on the basis of 17,340 informative SNPs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Genome-wide analysis of SNP diversity in the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection and cross-population selection signatures from wild to domesticated emmer transition (WEW to DEW) on the basis of 17,340 informative SNPs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="389" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, SNP-based diversity index (DI) for the main germplasm groups identified in the Global Tetraploid Wheat Collection: WEW, DEW, DWL and DWC. DI is reported as a centered 25 SNP-based average sliding window (single SNP step). Top and bottom 2.5% DI quantile distributions are highlighted as red- and blue-filled dots, respectively. <b>b</b>, Cross-population selection index metrics for the comparison between WEW and DEW. Selection metrics are provided for: diversity reduction index (DRI), divergence index (<i>F</i><sub>st</sub>), cross-population extended haplotype homozygosity (XP-EHH), multilocus test for allele frequency differentiation (XP-CLR) and haplotype-based differentiation test (hapFLK). For DRI, top and bottom 2.5% DI quantile distributions are highlighted as red- and blue-filled dots, respectively, while for the other selection metrics top 5% quantile distributions are highlighted as red-filled dots. The physical location of genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3#MOESM1">12</a>) and QTL confidence intervals relevant to domestication and breeding is reported.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Analysis of diversity and selection signatures in tetraploid wheat."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Analysis of diversity and selection signatures in tetraploid wheat.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0381-3/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0381-3/MediaObjects/41588_2019_381_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc"
